   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Compact disc
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_Disc&amp;redirect=no" title="Compact Disc">
       Compact Disc
      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation

    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       "CD" redirects here. For other uses, see
       <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/CD_(disambiguation)" title="CD (disambiguation)">
        CD (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <table class="infobox" style="width:22em">
       <caption>
        Compact Disc
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Compact_Disc_wordmark.svg">
          <img alt="Compact Disc wordmark.svg" data-file-height="203" data-file-width="410" height="74" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Compact_Disc_wordmark.svg/150px-Compact_Disc_wordmark.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Compact_Disc_wordmark.svg/225px-Compact_Disc_wordmark.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Compact_Disc_wordmark.svg/300px-Compact_Disc_wordmark.svg.png 2x" width="150"/>
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OD_Compact_disc.svg">
          <img alt="OD Compact disc.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/OD_Compact_disc.svg/220px-OD_Compact_disc.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/OD_Compact_disc.svg/330px-OD_Compact_disc.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/OD_Compact_disc.svg/440px-OD_Compact_disc.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
         </a>
         <div>
          The readable surface of a compact disc includes a spiral track wound tightly enough to cause light to
          <a href="/wiki/Diffraction_grating" title="Diffraction grating">
           diffract
          </a>
          into a full
          <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">
           visible spectrum
          </a>
          .
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Media type
        </th>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Optical_disc" title="Optical disc">
          Optical disc
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         <a href="/wiki/Content_format" title="Content format">
          Encoding
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         Various
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Capacity
        </th>
        <td>
         Typically up to 700
         <a href="/wiki/Mebibyte" title="Mebibyte">
          MiB
         </a>
         (up to 80 minutes audio)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Read
         <span class="nowrap">
         </span>
         mechanism
        </th>
        <td>
         780
         <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">
          nm
         </a>
         wavelength (
         <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
          infrared
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Red" title="Red">
          red
         </a>
         edge) semiconductor laser, 1200
         <a href="/wiki/Kibibit" title="Kibibit">
          Kibit
         </a>
         /s (1×)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Write
         <span class="nowrap">
         </span>
         mechanism
        </th>
        <td>
         780 nm laser with a focused beam more powerful than one used for reading, 1200 Kibit/s (1×)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         <a href="/wiki/International_standard" title="International standard">
          Standard
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc_Digital_Audio" title="Compact Disc Digital Audio">
          Red Book
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Developed
         <span class="nowrap">
         </span>
         by
        </th>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">
          Philips
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">
          Sony
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Usage
        </th>
        <td>
         Audio and data storage
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Extended
         <span class="nowrap">
         </span>
         from
        </th>

          LaserDisc
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>

         <span class="nowrap">

             <a href="/wiki/Professional_Disc" title="Professional Disc">
              Professional Disc
             </a>
            </li>
           </ul>
          </div>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>

        <td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">

       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Optical_disc_authoring" title="Template:Optical disc authoring">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Optical_disc_authoring" title="Template talk:Optical disc authoring">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Optical_disc_authoring&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Compact disc
       </b>
       (
       <b>
        CD
       </b>
       ) is a
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_media" title="Digital media">
        digital
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Optical_disc" title="Optical disc">
        optical disc
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_storage_device" title="Data storage device">
        data storage
       </a>
       format that was co-developed by
       <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">
        Philips
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">
        Sony
       </a>
       and released in 1982. The format was originally developed to store and play only sound recordings but was later adapted for storage of data (
       <a href="/wiki/CD-ROM" title="CD-ROM">
        CD-ROM
       </a>
       ). Several other formats were further derived from these, including write-once audio and data storage (
       <a href="/wiki/CD-R" title="CD-R">
        CD-R
       </a>
       ), rewritable media (
       <a href="/wiki/CD-RW" title="CD-RW">
        CD-RW
       </a>
       ), Video Compact Disc (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VCD" title="VCD">
        VCD
       </a>
       ), Super Video Compact Disc (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SVCD" title="SVCD">
        SVCD
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Photo_CD" title="Photo CD">
        Photo CD
       </a>
       , PictureCD,
       <a href="/wiki/Green_Book_(CD_standard)" title="Green Book (CD standard)">
        CD-i
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enhanced_Music_CD" title="Enhanced Music CD">
        Enhanced Music CD
       </a>
       . The first commercially available
       <a href="/wiki/CD_player" title="CD player">
        Audio CD player
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Sony_CDP-101" title="Sony CDP-101">
        Sony CDP-101
       </a>
       , was released October 1982 in
       <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">
        Japan
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Standard CDs have a diameter of 120 millimetres (4.7 in) and can hold up to about 80 minutes of uncompressed audio or about 700
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MiB" title="MiB">
        MiB
       </a>
       of data. The
       <a href="/wiki/Mini_CD" title="Mini CD">
        Mini CD
       </a>
       has various diameters ranging from 60 to 80 millimetres (2.4 to 3.1 in); they are sometimes used for CD singles, storing up to 24 minutes of audio, or delivering
       <a href="/wiki/Device_driver" title="Device driver">
        device drivers
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       At the time of the technology's introduction in 1982, a CD could store much more data than a
       <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">
        personal computer
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_drive" title="Hard drive">
        hard drive
       </a>
       , which would typically hold 10 MB. By 2010, hard drives commonly offered as much storage space as a thousand CDs, while their prices had plummeted to commodity level. In 2004, worldwide sales of audio CDs, CD-ROMs and CD-Rs reached about 30 billion discs. By 2007, 200 billion CDs had been sold worldwide.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-1_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-1-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       From the early 2000s CDs were increasingly being replaced by other forms of digital storage and distribution, with the result that by 2010 the number of audio CDs being sold in the U.S. had dropped about 50% from their peak; however, they remained one of the primary distribution methods for the music industry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-2_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-2-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2014, revenues from digital music services matched those from physical format sales for the first time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       American inventor
       <a href="/wiki/James_Russell_(inventor)" title="James Russell (inventor)">
        James T. Russell
       </a>
       has been credited with inventing the first system to record digital information on an optical
       <a href="/wiki/Transparency_and_translucency" title="Transparency and translucency">
        transparent
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Foil_(metal)" title="Foil (metal)">
        foil
       </a>
       that is lit from behind by a high-power halogen lamp.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Russell's patent application was first filed in 1966, and he was granted a patent in 1970. Following litigation, Sony and Philips licensed Russell's patents (then held by a Canadian company, Optical Recording Corp.) in the 1980s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The compact disc is an evolution of
       <a href="/wiki/LaserDisc" title="LaserDisc">
        LaserDisc
       </a>
       technology, where a focused
       <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">
        laser
       </a>
       beam is used that enables the high information density required for high-quality digital audio signals. Prototypes were developed by
       <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">
        Philips
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">
        Sony
       </a>
       independently in the late 1970s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although originally dismissed by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philips_Research" title="Philips Research">
        Philips Research
       </a>
       management as a trivial pursuit,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Immink2_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Immink2-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the CD became the primary focus for Philips as the
       <a href="/wiki/LaserDisc" title="LaserDisc">
        LaserDisc
       </a>
       format struggled.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndItsEnd_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AndItsEnd-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1979, Sony and Philips set up a joint task force of engineers to design a new digital audio disc. After a year of experimentation and discussion, the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_Books" title="Rainbow Books">
         Red Book
        </a>
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc_Digital_Audio" title="Compact Disc Digital Audio">
        CD-DA
       </a>
       standard was published in 1980. After their commercial release in 1982, compact discs and their players were extremely popular. Despite costing up to $1,000, over 400,000 CD players were sold in the United States between 1983 and 1984.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By 1988, CD sales in the United States surpassed those of vinyl LPs, and by 1992 CD sales surpassed those of prerecorded music cassette tapes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The success of the compact disc has been credited to the cooperation between
       <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">
        Philips
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">
        Sony
       </a>
       , which together agreed upon and developed compatible hardware. The unified design of the compact disc allowed consumers to purchase any disc or player from any company, and allowed the CD to dominate the at-home music market unchallenged.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_audio_laser-disc_prototypes">
        Digital audio laser-disc prototypes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Digital audio laser-disc prototypes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1974, L. Ottens, director of the audio division of
       <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">
        Philips
       </a>
       , started a small group with the aim to develop an analog
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Immink_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Immink-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       optical audio disc with a diameter of 20 cm (7.9 in) and a sound quality superior to that of the vinyl record.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, due to the unsatisfactory performance of the analog format, two Philips research engineers recommended a digital format in March 1974.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Immink_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Immink-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1977, Philips then established a laboratory with the mission of creating a digital audio disc. The diameter of Philips's prototype compact disc was set at 11.5 cm, the diagonal of an audio cassette.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Immink_16-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Immink-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peek_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-peek-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Heitaro_Nakajima" title="Heitaro Nakajima">
        Heitaro Nakajima
       </a>
       , who developed an early digital audio recorder within Japan's national public broadcasting organization
       <a href="/wiki/NHK" title="NHK">
        NHK
       </a>
       in 1970, became general manager of
       <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">
        Sony
       </a>
       's audio department in 1971. His team developed a digital
       <a href="/wiki/PCM_adaptor" title="PCM adaptor">
        PCM adaptor
       </a>
       audio tape recorder using a
       <a href="/wiki/Betamax" title="Betamax">
        Betamax
       </a>
       video recorder in 1973. After this, in 1974 the leap to storing digital audio on an optical disc was easily made.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">
        Sony
       </a>
       first publicly demonstrated an optical digital audio disc in September 1976. A year later, in September 1977, Sony showed the press a 30 cm disc that could play 60 minutes of digital audio (44,100 Hz sampling rate and 16-bit resolution) using
       <a href="/wiki/Run-length_limited" title="Run-length limited">
        MFM
       </a>
       modulation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SonyHistorical_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SonyHistorical-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In September 1978, the company demonstrated an optical digital audio disc with a 150-minute playing time, 44,056 Hz sampling rate, 16-bit linear resolution, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-interleaved_Reed-Solomon_coding" title="Cross-interleaved Reed-Solomon coding">
        cross-interleaved
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Error_correction" title="Error correction">
        error correction
       </a>
       code—specifications similar to those later settled upon for the standard compact disc format in 1980. Technical details of Sony's digital audio disc were presented during the 62nd
       <a href="/wiki/Audio_Engineering_Society" title="Audio Engineering Society">
        AES
       </a>
       Convention, held on 13–16 March 1979, in
       <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">
        Brussels
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SonyHistorical_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SonyHistorical-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sony's AES technical paper was published on 1 March 1979. A week later, on 8 March, Philips publicly demonstrated a prototype of an optical digital audio disc at a press conference called "Philips Introduce Compact Disc"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC6950933_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BBC6950933-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Eindhoven" title="Eindhoven">
        Eindhoven
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">
        Netherlands
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhilipsHistorical_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PhilipsHistorical-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Collaboration_and_standardization">
        Collaboration and standardization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Collaboration and standardization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schouhamerimmink.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="498" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Schouhamerimmink.jpg/170px-Schouhamerimmink.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Schouhamerimmink.jpg/255px-Schouhamerimmink.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Schouhamerimmink.jpg/340px-Schouhamerimmink.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schouhamerimmink.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Dutch inventor and Philips chief engineer
         <a href="/wiki/Kees_Schouhamer_Immink" title="Kees Schouhamer Immink">
          Kees Schouhamer Immink
         </a>
         was part of the team that produced the standard compact disc in 1980
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Sony executive
       <a href="/wiki/Norio_Ohga" title="Norio Ohga">
        Norio Ohga
       </a>
       , later CEO and chairman of Sony, and
       <a href="/wiki/Heitaro_Nakajima" title="Heitaro Nakajima">
        Heitaro Nakajima
       </a>
       were convinced of the format's commercial potential and pushed further development despite widespread skepticism.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohgaobituary_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ohgaobituary-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As a result, in 1979, Sony and Philips set up a joint task force of engineers to design a new digital audio disc. Led by engineers
       <a href="/wiki/Kees_Schouhamer_Immink" title="Kees Schouhamer Immink">
        Kees Schouhamer Immink
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Toshitada_Doi" title="Toshitada Doi">
        Toshitada Doi
       </a>
       , the research pushed forward
       <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">
        laser
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Optical_disc" title="Optical disc">
        optical disc
       </a>
       technology.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC6950933_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-BBC6950933-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After a year of experimentation and discussion, the task force produced the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_Books" title="Rainbow Books">
         Red Book
        </a>
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc_Digital_Audio" title="Compact Disc Digital Audio">
        CD-DA
       </a>
       standard. First published in 1980, the standard was formally adopted by the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">
        IEC
       </a>
       as an international standard in 1987, with various amendments becoming part of the standard in 1996.
      </p>
      <p>
       Philips coined the term
       <i>
        compact disc
       </i>
       in line with another audio product, the
       <a href="/wiki/Compact_Cassette" title="Compact Cassette">
        Compact Cassette
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peek_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-peek-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and contributed the general
       <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">
        manufacturing
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_process" title="Industrial process">
        process
       </a>
       , based on video LaserDisc technology. Philips also contributed
       <a href="/wiki/Eight-to-fourteen_modulation" title="Eight-to-fourteen modulation">
        eight-to-fourteen modulation
       </a>
       (EFM), which offers a certain resilience to defects such as scratches and fingerprints, while Sony contributed the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Error-correction" title="Error-correction">
        error-correction
       </a>
       method,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-Interleaved_Reed-Solomon_Coding" title="Cross-Interleaved Reed-Solomon Coding">
        CIRC
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Compact Disc Story
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Immink_16-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Immink-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       told by a former member of the task force, gives background information on the many technical decisions made, including the choice of the sampling frequency, playing time, and disc diameter. The task force consisted of around four to eight persons,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Immink2_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Immink2-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knopper_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Knopper-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       though according to Philips, the compact disc was "invented collectively by a large group of people working as a team."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhilDoss_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PhilDoss-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_launch_and_adoption">
        Initial launch and adoption
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Initial launch and adoption">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Philips established the Polydor Pressing Operations plant in
       <a href="/wiki/Langenhagen" title="Langenhagen">
        Langenhagen
       </a>
       near
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hannover" title="Hannover">
        Hannover
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       , and quickly passed a series of milestones.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The first
        <i>
         test pressing
        </i>
        was of a recording of
        <a href="/wiki/Richard_Strauss" title="Richard Strauss">
         Richard Strauss
        </a>
        's
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/An_Alpine_Symphony" title="An Alpine Symphony">
          Eine Alpensinfonie
         </a>
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         An Alpine Symphony
        </i>
        ) played by the
        <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Philharmonic" title="Berlin Philharmonic">
         Berlin Philharmonic
        </a>
        and conducted by
        <a href="/wiki/Herbert_von_Karajan" title="Herbert von Karajan">
         Herbert von Karajan
        </a>
        , who had been enlisted as an ambassador for the format in 1979.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-3_26-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-3-26">
          [26]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The first
        <i>
         public demonstration
        </i>
        was on the
        <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">
         BBC
        </a>
        television programme
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Tomorrow%27s_World" title="Tomorrow's World">
          Tomorrow's World
         </a>
        </i>
        in 1981, when the
        <a href="/wiki/Bee_Gees" title="Bee Gees">
         Bee Gees
        </a>
        ' album
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Living_Eyes_(Bee_Gees_album)" title="Living Eyes (Bee Gees album)">
          Living Eyes
         </a>
        </i>
        (1981) was played.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-4_27-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-4-27">
          [27]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The first
        <i>
         commercial
        </i>
        compact disc was produced on 17 August 1982. It was
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Visitors_(ABBA_album)" title="The Visitors (ABBA album)">
          The Visitors
         </a>
        </i>
        (1981) by
        <a href="/wiki/ABBA" title="ABBA">
         ABBA
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-5_28-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-5-28">
          [28]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The first 50 titles were
        <i>
         released
        </i>
        in Japan on 1 October 1982,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-6_29-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-6-29">
          [29]
         </a>
        </sup>
        the very first of which was a rerelease of the
        <a href="/wiki/Billy_Joel" title="Billy Joel">
         Billy Joel
        </a>
        album
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/52nd_Street_(album)" title="52nd Street (album)">
          52nd Street
         </a>
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
         <a href="#cite_note-30">
          [30]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The first CD played on BBC Radio was in October 1982 on BBC Radio Scotland (Jimmy Mack programme, Followed by Ken Bruce and Eddie Mair all BBC Scotland), with the first CD
        <i>
         played
        </i>
        on UK independent radio station shortly after (Radio Forth, Jay Crawford Show). The CD was Dire Straits,
        <i>
         Love over Gold
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
         <a href="#cite_note-31">
          [31]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The Japanese launch was followed in March 1983 by the introduction of CD players and discs to Europe
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and North America (where CBS Records released sixteen titles).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G&amp;M_1983-03-05_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-G&amp;M_1983-03-05-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This 1983 event is often seen as the "Big Bang"
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch">
          <span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (October 2017)">
           by whom?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       of the digital audio revolution. The new audio disc was enthusiastically received, especially in the early-adopting
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_classical_music" title="European classical music">
        classical music
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Audiophile" title="Audiophile">
        audiophile
       </a>
       communities, and its handling quality received particular praise. As the price of players gradually came down, and with the introduction of the portable
       <a href="/wiki/Discman" title="Discman">
        Discman
       </a>
       the CD began to gain popularity in the larger popular and rock music markets. One of the first CD markets was devoted to reissuing popular music whose commercial potential was already proven. An advantage of the format was the ability to produce and market boxed sets and multi-volume collections.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndItsEnd_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-AndItsEnd-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first artist to sell a million copies on CD was
       <a href="/wiki/Dire_Straits" title="Dire Straits">
        Dire Straits
       </a>
       , with their 1985 album
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Brothers_in_Arms_(album)" title="Brothers in Arms (album)">
         Brothers in Arms
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-7_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-7-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first major artist to have his entire catalogue converted to CD was
       <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie" title="David Bowie">
        David Bowie
       </a>
       , whose 15 studio albums were made available by
       <a href="/wiki/RCA_Records" title="RCA Records">
        RCA Records
       </a>
       in February 1985, along with four greatest hits albums.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-8_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-8-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On February 26, 1987, the first four UK albums by
       <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">
        The Beatles
       </a>
       were released in mono on compact disc.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeatlesCD_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BeatlesCD-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1988, 400 million CDs were manufactured by 50 pressing plants around the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-9_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-9-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Further_development_and_decline">
        Further development and decline
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Further development and decline">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sony_CD_Walkman_D-E330.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2304" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Sony_CD_Walkman_D-E330.jpg/220px-Sony_CD_Walkman_D-E330.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Sony_CD_Walkman_D-E330.jpg/330px-Sony_CD_Walkman_D-E330.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Sony_CD_Walkman_D-E330.jpg/440px-Sony_CD_Walkman_D-E330.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sony_CD_Walkman_D-E330.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Sony CD Walkman D-E330
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The CD was planned to be the successor of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vinyl_record" title="Vinyl record">
        vinyl record
       </a>
       for playing music, rather than primarily as a data storage medium. From its origins as a musical format, CDs have grown to encompass other applications. In 1983, following the CD's introduction,
       <a href="/wiki/Kees_Schouhamer_Immink" title="Kees Schouhamer Immink">
        Immink
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Braat" title="Joseph Braat">
        Braat
       </a>
       presented the first experiments with erasable compact discs during the 73rd
       <a href="/wiki/Audio_Engineering_Society" title="Audio Engineering Society">
        AES
       </a>
       Convention.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In June 1985, the computer-readable
       <a href="/wiki/CD-ROM" title="CD-ROM">
        CD-ROM
       </a>
       (read-only memory) and, in 1990,
       <a href="/wiki/CD-R" title="CD-R">
        CD-Recordable
       </a>
       were introduced, also developed by both Sony and Philips.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-10_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-10-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Recordable CDs were a new alternative to tape for recording music and copying music albums without defects introduced in compression used in other digital recording methods. Other newer video formats such as
       <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">
        DVD
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray" title="Blu-ray">
        Blu-ray
       </a>
       use the same physical geometry as CD, and most DVD and Blu-ray players are
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Backward_compatible" title="Backward compatible">
        backward compatible
       </a>
       with audio CD.
      </p>
      <p>
       By the early 2000s, the CD player had largely replaced the
       <a href="/wiki/Compact_Cassette" title="Compact Cassette">
        audio cassette
       </a>
       player as standard equipment in new automobiles, with 2010 being the final model year for any car in the United States to have a factory-equipped cassette player.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20120718_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nyt20120718-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With the increasing popularity of portable digital audio players, such as mobile phones, and solid state music storage, CD players are being phased out of automobiles in favor of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minijack" title="Minijack">
        minijack
       </a>
       auxiliary inputs, wired connection to USB devices and wireless
       <a href="/wiki/Bluetooth" title="Bluetooth">
        Bluetooth
       </a>
       connection.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Meanwhile, with the advent and popularity of
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_distribution" title="Digital distribution">
        Internet-based distribution
       </a>
       of files in
       <a href="/wiki/Lossy_compression" title="Lossy compression">
        lossily-compressed
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audio_format" title="Audio format">
        audio formats
       </a>
       such as
       <a href="/wiki/MP3" title="MP3">
        MP3
       </a>
       , sales of CDs began to decline in the 2000s. For example, between 2000 and 2008, despite overall growth in music sales and one anomalous year of increase, major-label CD sales declined overall by 20%,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-11_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-11-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although independent and DIY music sales may be tracking better according to figures released 30 March 2009, and CDs still continue to sell greatly.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-12_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-12-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 2012, CDs and DVDs made up only 34 percent of music sales in the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By 2015
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , only 24% of music in the United States was purchased on physical media, ⅔ of this consisting of CDs;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       however, in the same year in Japan, over 80% of music was bought on CDs and other physical formats.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite the rapidly declining sales year-over-year, the pervasiveness of the technology remained for a time, with companies placing CDs in pharmacies, supermarkets, and filling station convenience stores targeting buyers least able to utilize Internet-based distribution.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndItsEnd_11-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-AndItsEnd-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, in 2018,
       <a href="/wiki/Best_Buy" title="Best Buy">
        Best Buy
       </a>
       announced plans to end CD sales in its stores on July 1, 2018, with
       <a href="/wiki/Target_Corporation" title="Target Corporation">
        Target Corporation
       </a>
       planning to only offer CDs on a
       <a href="/wiki/Consignment" title="Consignment">
        consignment
       </a>
       basis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bestverge_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bestverge-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_accolades">
        Awards and accolades
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Awards and accolades">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Sony and Philips received praise for the development of the compact disc from professional organizations. These awards include
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Technical
        <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">
         Grammy Award
        </a>
        for Sony and Philips, 1998.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grammy_48-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-grammy-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IEEE" title="IEEE">
         IEEE
        </a>
        Milestone award, 2009, for Philips only with the citation: "On 8 March 1979, N.V. Philips' Gloeilampenfabrieken demonstrated for the international press a Compact Disc Audio Player. The demonstration showed that it is possible by using digital optical recording and playback to reproduce audio signals with superb stereo quality. This research at Philips established the technical standard for digital optical recording systems."
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEE_CD_Milestone_49-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-IEEE_CD_Milestone-49">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_details">
        Physical details
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Physical details">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           needs additional citations for
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            verification
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Relevant discussion may be found on the
           <a href="/wiki/Talk:Compact_disc#Numbers_in_the_Physical_Details_section" title="Talk:Compact disc">
            talk page
           </a>
           . Please help
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit">
            improve this article
           </a>
           by
           <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">
            adding citations to reliable sources
           </a>
           . Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (May 2016)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CD_layers.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/CD_layers.svg/220px-CD_layers.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/CD_layers.svg/330px-CD_layers.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/CD_layers.svg/440px-CD_layers.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CD_layers.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Diagram of CD layers.
         <div>
          <ol style="list-style-type:upper-alpha">
           <li>
            A polycarbonate disc layer has the data encoded by using bumps.
           </li>
           <li>
            A shiny layer reflects the laser.
           </li>
           <li>
            A layer of lacquer protects the shiny layer.
           </li>
           <li>
            Artwork is
            <a href="/wiki/Screen_printing" title="Screen printing">
             screen printed
            </a>
            on the top of the disc.
           </li>
           <li>
            A laser beam reads the CD and is reflected back to a sensor, which converts it into electronic data
           </li>
          </ol>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A CD is made from 1.2 millimetres (0.047 in) thick,
       <a href="/wiki/Polycarbonate" title="Polycarbonate">
        polycarbonate
       </a>
       plastic and weighs 15–20 grams.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-13_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-13-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS">
          <span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (May 2016)">
           better source needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       From the center outward, components are: the center spindle hole (15 mm), the first-transition area (clamping ring), the clamping area (stacking ring), the second-transition area (mirror band), the program (data) area, and the rim. The inner program area occupies a radius from 25 to 58 mm.
      </p>
      <p>
       A thin layer of aluminium or, more rarely,
       <a href="/wiki/Gold_compact_disc" title="Gold compact disc">
        gold
       </a>
       is applied to the surface, making it reflective. The metal is protected by a film of lacquer normally
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin_coated" title="Spin coated">
        spin coated
       </a>
       directly on the reflective layer. The label is printed on the lacquer layer, usually by
       <a href="/wiki/Screen_printing" title="Screen printing">
        screen printing
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Offset_printing" title="Offset printing">
        offset printing
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       CD data is represented as tiny indentations known as "pits", encoded in a spiral track moulded into the top of the polycarbonate layer. The areas between pits are known as "lands". Each pit is approximately 100
       <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">
        nm
       </a>
       deep by 500 nm wide, and varies from 850 nm to 3.5
       <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">
        µm
       </a>
       in length.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The distance between the tracks, the pitch, is 1.6 µm.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEC908_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IEC908-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEC10149_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IEC10149-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A motor within the CD player spins the disc to a scanning velocity of 1.2–1.4 m/s (
       <a href="/wiki/Constant_linear_velocity" title="Constant linear velocity">
        constant linear velocity
       </a>
       ) – equivalent to approximately 500 RPM at the inside of the disc, and approximately 200 RPM at the outside edge. (A disc played from beginning to end slows its rotation rate during playback.)
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_CD_DVD_HDDVD_BD.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="512" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Comparison_CD_DVD_HDDVD_BD.svg/300px-Comparison_CD_DVD_HDDVD_BD.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Comparison_CD_DVD_HDDVD_BD.svg/450px-Comparison_CD_DVD_HDDVD_BD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Comparison_CD_DVD_HDDVD_BD.svg/600px-Comparison_CD_DVD_HDDVD_BD.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_CD_DVD_HDDVD_BD.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Comparison of various optical storage media
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The program area is 86.05 cm
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       and the length of the recordable spiral is (86.05 cm
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       / 1.6 µm) = 5.38 km. With a scanning speed of 1.2 m/s, the playing time is 74 minutes, or 650 MiB of data on a CD-ROM. A disc with data packed slightly more densely is tolerated by most players (though some old ones fail). Using a linear velocity of 1.2 m/s and a narrower track pitch of 1.5 µm increases the playing time to 80 minutes, and data capacity to 700 MiB.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CD_Pits_at_6.25x_Magnification.jpg">
         <img alt="This is a photomicrograph of the pits at the inner edge of a CD-ROM; 2 second exposure under visible fluorescent light." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="898" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/CD_Pits_at_6.25x_Magnification.jpg/220px-CD_Pits_at_6.25x_Magnification.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/CD_Pits_at_6.25x_Magnification.jpg/330px-CD_Pits_at_6.25x_Magnification.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/CD_Pits_at_6.25x_Magnification.jpg/440px-CD_Pits_at_6.25x_Magnification.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CD_Pits_at_6.25x_Magnification.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The pits in a CD are 500
         <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">
          nm
         </a>
         wide, between 830 nm and 3,000 nm long and 150 nm deep
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A CD is read by focusing a 780 nm
       <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">
        wavelength
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_infrared" title="Near infrared">
        near infrared
       </a>
       )
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiconductor_laser" title="Semiconductor laser">
        semiconductor laser
       </a>
       housed within the CD player, through the bottom of the polycarbonate layer. The change in height between pits and lands results in a difference in the way the light is reflected. By measuring the intensity change with a
       <a href="/wiki/Photodiode" title="Photodiode">
        photodiode
       </a>
       , the data can be read from the disc. In order to accommodate the spiral pattern of data, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiconductor_laser" title="Semiconductor laser">
        semiconductor laser
       </a>
       is placed on a swing arm within the disc tray of any CD player. This swing arm allows the laser to read information from the centre to the edge of a disc, without having to interrupt the spinning of the disc itself.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CDM210_cd_laufwerk.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/CDM210_cd_laufwerk.jpg/220px-CDM210_cd_laufwerk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/CDM210_cd_laufwerk.jpg/330px-CDM210_cd_laufwerk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/CDM210_cd_laufwerk.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CDM210_cd_laufwerk.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Philips CDM210 CD Drive
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The pits and lands do not directly represent the zeros and ones of
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_data" title="Binary data">
        binary data
       </a>
       . Instead,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-return-to-zero,_inverted" title="Non-return-to-zero, inverted">
        non-return-to-zero, inverted
       </a>
       encoding is used: a change from pit to land or land to pit indicates a one, while no change indicates a series of zeros. There must be at least two and no more than ten zeros between each one, which is defined by the length of the pit. This in turn is decoded by reversing the
       <a href="/wiki/Eight-to-fourteen_modulation" title="Eight-to-fourteen modulation">
        eight-to-fourteen modulation
       </a>
       used in mastering the disc, and then reversing the
       <a href="/wiki/Cross-interleaved_Reed%E2%80%93Solomon_coding" title="Cross-interleaved Reed–Solomon coding">
        cross-interleaved Reed–Solomon coding
       </a>
       , finally revealing the raw data stored on the disc. These encoding techniques (defined in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc_Digital_Audio#Data_encoding" title="Compact Disc Digital Audio">
         Red Book
        </a>
       </i>
       ) were originally designed for
       <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc_Digital_Audio" title="Compact Disc Digital Audio">
        CD Digital Audio
       </a>
       , but they later became a standard for almost all CD formats (such as
       <a href="/wiki/CD-ROM" title="CD-ROM">
        CD-ROM
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Integrity">
        Integrity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Integrity">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       CDs are susceptible to damage during handling and from environmental exposure. Pits are much closer to the label side of a disc, enabling defects and contaminants on the clear side to be out of focus during playback. Consequently, CDs are more likely to suffer damage on the label side of the disc. Scratches on the clear side can be repaired by refilling them with similar refractive plastic or by careful polishing. The edges of CDs are sometimes incompletely sealed, allowing gases and liquids to corrode the metal reflective layer and to interfere with the focus of the laser on the pits.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clir_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-clir-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The fungus
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Geotrichum_candidum" title="Geotrichum candidum">
         Geotrichum candidum
        </a>
       </i>
       has been found—under conditions of high heat and humidity—to consume the polycarbonate plastic and aluminium found in CDs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Disc_shapes_and_diameters">
        Disc shapes and diameters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Disc shapes and diameters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_disk_storage.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Comparison_disk_storage.svg/300px-Comparison_disk_storage.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Comparison_disk_storage.svg/450px-Comparison_disk_storage.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Comparison_disk_storage.svg/600px-Comparison_disk_storage.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_disk_storage.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Comparison of several forms of disk storage showing tracks (not-to-scale); green denotes start and red denotes end.
         <br/>
         * Some CD-R(W) and DVD-R(W)/DVD+R(W) recorders operate in ZCLV, CAA or CAV modes.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The digital data on a CD begins at the center of the disc and proceeds toward the edge, which allows adaptation to the different size formats available. Standard CDs are available in two sizes. By far, the most common is 120 millimetres (4.7 in) in diameter, with a 74- or 80-minute audio capacity and a 650 or 700 MiB (737,280,000-byte) data capacity. This capacity was reportedly specified by Sony executive
       <a href="/wiki/Norio_Ohga" title="Norio Ohga">
        Norio Ohga
       </a>
       in May 1980 so as to be able to contain the entirety of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beethoven%27s_Ninth_Symphony" title="Beethoven's Ninth Symphony">
        Beethoven's Ninth Symphony
       </a>
       on one disc.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohgaobituary_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ohgaobituary-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is a myth according to
       <a href="/wiki/Kees_Schouhamer_Immink" title="Kees Schouhamer Immink">
        Kees Immink
       </a>
       , as the code format had not yet been decided in May 1980.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Immink2_10-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Immink2-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The adoption of
       <a href="/wiki/Eight-to-fourteen_modulation" title="Eight-to-fourteen modulation">
        EFM
       </a>
       one month later would have allowed a playing time of 97 minutes for 120 mm diameter or 74 minutes for a disc as small as 100 mm.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 120 mm diameter has been adopted by subsequent formats, including
       <a href="/wiki/Super_Audio_CD" title="Super Audio CD">
        Super Audio CD
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">
        DVD
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/HD_DVD" title="HD DVD">
        HD DVD
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" title="Blu-ray Disc">
        Blu-ray Disc
       </a>
       . The 80 mm diameter discs ("
       <a href="/wiki/Mini_CD" title="Mini CD">
        Mini CDs
       </a>
       ") can hold up to 24 minutes of music or 210 MiB.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>

        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
