         120 mm
        </td>
        <td>
         74–80 min
        </td>
        <td>
         650–700
         <a href="/wiki/Mebibyte" title="Mebibyte">
          MiB
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         Standard size
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         80 mm
        </td>
        <td>
         21–24 min
        </td>
        <td>
         185–210 MiB
        </td>
        <td>
         Mini-CD size
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         80x54 mm – 80x64 mm
        </td>
        <td>
         ~6 min
        </td>
        <td>
         10-65 MiB
        </td>
        <td>
         "Business card" size
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Logical_format">
        Logical format
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Logical format">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Audio_CD">
        Audio CD
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Audio CD">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc_Digital_Audio" title="Compact Disc Digital Audio">
        Compact Disc Digital Audio
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The logical format of an audio CD (officially Compact Disc Digital Audio or CD-DA) is described in a document produced in 1980 by the format's joint creators, Sony and Philips. The document is known colloquially as the
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc_Digital_Audio" title="Compact Disc Digital Audio">
        CD-DA
       </a>
       after the colour of its cover. The format is a two-channel 16-bit
       <a href="/wiki/Pulse-code_modulation" title="Pulse-code modulation">
        PCM
       </a>
       encoding at a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/44.1_kHz" title="44.1 kHz">
        44.1 kHz
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sampling_rate" title="Sampling rate">
        sampling rate
       </a>
       per channel.
       <a href="/wiki/Four-channel_Compact_Disc_Digital_Audio" title="Four-channel Compact Disc Digital Audio">
        Four-channel sound
       </a>
       was to be an allowable option within the
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       format, but has never been implemented.
       <a href="/wiki/Monaural" title="Monaural">
        Monaural
       </a>
       audio has no existing standard on a
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       CD; thus, mono source material is usually presented as two identical channels in a standard
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       stereo track (i.e.,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mono_sound#Mirrored_mono" title="Mono sound">
        mirrored mono
       </a>
       ); an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MP3_CD" title="MP3 CD">
        MP3 CD
       </a>
       , however, can have audio file formats with mono sound.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/CD-Text" title="CD-Text">
        CD-Text
       </a>
       is an extension of the
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       specification for audio CD that allows for storage of additional text information (e.g., album name, song name, artist) on a standards-compliant audio CD. The information is stored either in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lead-in_(CD)" title="Lead-in (CD)">
        lead-in area
       </a>
       of the CD, where there is roughly five kilobytes of space available, or in the
       <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc_subcode" title="Compact Disc subcode">
        subcode
       </a>
       channels R to W on the disc, which can store about 31 megabytes.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compact_Disc_%2B_Graphics" title="Compact Disc + Graphics">
        Compact Disc + Graphics
       </a>
       is a special audio compact disc that contains graphics data in addition to the audio data on the disc. The disc can be played on a regular audio CD player, but when played on a special CD+G player, it can output a graphics signal (typically, the CD+G player is hooked up to a television set or a computer monitor); these graphics are almost exclusively used to display lyrics on a television set for
       <a href="/wiki/Karaoke" title="Karaoke">
        karaoke
       </a>
       performers to sing along with. The CD+G format takes advantage of the channels R through W. These six bits store the graphics information.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CD_%2B_Extended_Graphics" title="CD + Extended Graphics">
        CD + Extended Graphics
       </a>
       (CD+EG, also known as CD+XG) is an improved variant of the
       <a href="#CD_+_Graphics">
        Compact Disc + Graphics
       </a>
       (CD+G) format. Like CD+G, CD+EG utilizes basic CD-ROM features to display text and video information in addition to the music being played. This extra data is stored in
       <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc_subcode" title="Compact Disc subcode">
        subcode
       </a>
       channels R-W. Very few, if any, CD+EG discs have been published.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Super_Audio_CD">
        Super Audio CD
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Super Audio CD">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Super_Audio_CD" title="Super Audio CD">
        Super Audio CD
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Super Audio CD (SACD) is a high-resolution read-only
       <a href="/wiki/Optical_disc" title="Optical disc">
        optical
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Audio_storage" title="Audio storage">
        audio disc
       </a>
       format that was designed to provide
       <a href="/wiki/High_fidelity" title="High fidelity">
        higher fidelity
       </a>
       digital audio reproduction than the
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       . Introduced in 1999, it was developed by Sony and Philips, the same companies that created the
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       . SACD was in a
       <a href="/wiki/Format_war" title="Format war">
        format war
       </a>
       with
       <a href="/wiki/DVD-Audio" title="DVD-Audio">
        DVD-Audio
       </a>
       , but neither has replaced audio CDs. The SACD standard is referred to the
       <i>
        Scarlet Book
       </i>
       standard.
      </p>
      <p>
       Titles in the SACD format can be issued as hybrid discs; these discs contain the SACD audio stream as well as a standard audio CD layer which is playable in standard CD players, thus making them
       <a href="/wiki/Backward_compatibility" title="Backward compatibility">
        backward compatible
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="CD-MIDI">
        CD-MIDI
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: CD-MIDI">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       CD-
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musical_Instrument_Digital_Interface" title="Musical Instrument Digital Interface">
        MIDI
       </a>
       is a format used to store music-performance data, which upon playback is performed by electronic instruments that synthesize the audio. Hence, unlike the original
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       CD-DA, these recordings are not digitally sampled audio recordings. The CD-MIDI format is defined as an extension of the original
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="CD-ROM">
        CD-ROM
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: CD-ROM">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/CD-ROM" title="CD-ROM">
        CD-ROM
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       For the first few years of its existence, the CD was a medium used purely for audio. However, in 1988, the
       <i>
        Yellow Book
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/CD-ROM" title="CD-ROM">
        CD-ROM
       </a>
       standard was established by Sony and Philips, which defined a non-volatile optical data
       <a href="/wiki/Computer_data_storage" title="Computer data storage">
        computer data storage
       </a>
       medium using the same physical format as audio compact discs, readable by a computer with a CD-ROM drive.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Video_CD_.28VCD.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Video_CD_(VCD)">
        Video CD (VCD)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Video CD (VCD)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Video_CD" title="Video CD">
        Video CD
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Video CD (VCD, View CD, and Compact Disc digital video) is a standard
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_data" title="Digital data">
        digital
       </a>
       format for storing video media on a CD. VCDs are playable in dedicated VCD players, most modern
       <a href="/wiki/DVD-Video" title="DVD-Video">
        DVD-Video
       </a>
       players, personal computers, and some video game consoles.
      </p>
      <p>
       The VCD standard was created in 1993 by Sony, Philips,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panasonic_Corporation" title="Panasonic Corporation">
        Matsushita
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/JVC" title="JVC">
        JVC
       </a>
       and is referred to as the
       <i>
        White Book
       </i>
       standard.
      </p>
      <p>
       Overall picture quality is intended to be comparable to
       <a href="/wiki/VHS" title="VHS">
        VHS
       </a>
       video. Poorly compressed VCD video can sometimes be lower quality than VHS video, but VCD exhibits block artifacts rather than analog noise and does not deteriorate further with each use.
      </p>
      <p>
       352x240 (or
       <a href="/wiki/Source_Input_Format" title="Source Input Format">
        SIF
       </a>
       ) resolution was chosen because it is half the vertical and half the horizontal resolution of NTSC video. 352x288 is similarly one quarter PAL/SECAM resolution. This approximates the (overall) resolution of an analog VHS tape, which, although it has double the number of (vertical) scan lines, has a much lower horizontal resolution.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Super_Video_CD">
        Super Video CD
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Super Video CD">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Super_Video_CD" title="Super Video CD">
        Super Video CD
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Super Video CD (Super Video Compact Disc or SVCD) is a format used for storing video media on standard compact discs. SVCD was intended as a successor to VCD and an alternative to DVD-Video and falls somewhere between both in terms of technical capability and picture quality.
      </p>
      <p>
       SVCD has two thirds the
       <a href="/wiki/Display_resolution" title="Display resolution">
        resolution
       </a>
       of DVD, and over 2.7 times the resolution of VCD. One CD-R disc can hold up to 60 minutes of standard quality SVCD-format video. While no specific limit on SVCD video length is mandated by the specification, one must lower the video bit rate, and therefore quality, to accommodate very long videos. It is usually difficult to fit much more than 100 minutes of video onto one SVCD without incurring significant quality loss, and many hardware players are unable to play video with an instantaneous bit rate lower than 300 to 600
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilobits" title="Kilobits">
        kilobits
       </a>
       per second.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Photo_CD">
        Photo CD
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Photo CD">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Photo_CD" title="Photo CD">
        Photo CD
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Photo CD is a system designed by
       <a href="/wiki/Kodak" title="Kodak">
        Kodak
       </a>
       for digitizing and storing photos on a CD. Launched in 1992, the discs were designed to hold nearly 100 high-quality images, scanned prints and slides using special proprietary encoding. Photo CDs are defined in the
       <i>
        Beige Book
       </i>
       and conform to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CD-ROM_XA" title="CD-ROM XA">
        CD-ROM XA
       </a>
       and CD-i Bridge specifications as well. They are intended to play on CD-i players, Photo CD players and any computer with suitable software (irrespective of
       <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">
        operating system
       </a>
       ). The images can also be printed out on photographic paper with a special Kodak machine. This format is not to be confused with Kodak
       <a href="/wiki/Picture_CD" title="Picture CD">
        Picture CD
       </a>
       , which is a consumer product in CD-ROM format.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="CD-i">
        CD-i
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: CD-i">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Philips_CD-i" title="Philips CD-i">
        Philips CD-i
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Philips
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Green_Book_(CD_standard)" title="Green Book (CD standard)">
         Green Book
        </a>
       </i>
       specifies a standard for interactive multimedia compact discs designed for
       <a href="/wiki/Philips_CD-i" title="Philips CD-i">
        CD-i
       </a>
       players (1993). CD-i discs can contain audio tracks which can be played on regular
       <a href="/wiki/CD_player" title="CD player">
        CD players
       </a>
       , but CD-i discs are not compatible with most
       <a href="/wiki/CD-ROM" title="CD-ROM">
        CD-ROM
       </a>
       drives and software. The
       <a href="/wiki/CD-i_Ready" title="CD-i Ready">
        CD-i Ready
       </a>
       specification was later created to improve compatibility with audio CD players, and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CD-i_Bridge" title="CD-i Bridge">
        CD-i Bridge
       </a>
       specification was added to create CD-i compatible discs that can be accessed by regular CD-ROM drives.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="CD-i_Ready">
        CD-i Ready
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: CD-i Ready">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/CD-i_Ready" title="CD-i Ready">
        CD-i Ready
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Philips defined a format similar to CD-i called
       <a href="/wiki/CD-i_Ready" title="CD-i Ready">
        CD-i Ready
       </a>
       , which puts CD-i software and data into the
       <a href="/wiki/Pregap#Computer_data_in_pregap" title="Pregap">
        pregap
       </a>
       of track 1. This format was supposed to be more compatible with older audio CD players.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Enhanced_Music_CD_.28CD.2B.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Enhanced_Music_CD_(CD+)">
        Enhanced Music CD (CD+)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Enhanced Music CD (CD+)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Blue_Book_(CD_standard)" title="Blue Book (CD standard)">
        Blue Book (CD standard)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Enhanced Music CD, also known as CD Extra or CD Plus, is a format which combines
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CD-DA" title="CD-DA">
        audio tracks
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/CD-ROM" title="CD-ROM">
        data tracks
       </a>
       on the same disc by putting audio tracks in a first
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Session_(CD)" title="Session (CD)">
        session
       </a>
       and data in a second session. It was developed by Philips and Sony, and it is defined in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Blue_Book_(CD_standard)" title="Blue Book (CD standard)">
         Blue Book
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vinyl_Disc">
        Vinyl Disc
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Vinyl Disc">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/VinylDisc" title="VinylDisc">
        VinylDisc
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Vinyl Disc is the hybrid of a standard audio CD and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gramophone_record" title="Gramophone record">
        vinyl record
       </a>
       . The vinyl layer on the disc's label side can hold approximately three minutes of music.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Manufacture">
        Manufacture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Manufacture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CD_manufacturing" title="CD manufacturing">
        CD manufacturing
       </a>
      </div>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-disputed" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <img alt="System-search.svg" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="45" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/System-search.svg/45px-System-search.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/System-search.svg/68px-System-search.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/System-search.svg/90px-System-search.svg.png 2x" width="45"/>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section's
          <b>
           factual accuracy is
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute">
            disputed
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Relevant discussion may be found on
           <a href="/wiki/Talk:Compact_disc#Dispute" title="Talk:Compact disc">
            Talk:Compact disc
           </a>
           . Please help to ensure that disputed statements are
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">
            reliably sourced
           </a>
           .
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (April 2018)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Compact_disk_data_layer_2d_3d.PNG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="611" data-file-width="327" height="411" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Compact_disk_data_layer_2d_3d.PNG/220px-Compact_disk_data_layer_2d_3d.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2c/Compact_disk_data_layer_2d_3d.PNG 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Compact_disk_data_layer_2d_3d.PNG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Individual pits are visible on the micrometre scale
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Replicated CDs are mass-produced initially using a hydraulic press. Small granules of heated raw polycarbonate plastic are fed into the press. A screw forces the liquefied plastic into the mold cavity. The mold closes with a metal stamper in contact with the disc surface. The plastic is allowed to cool and harden. Once opened, the disc substrate is removed from the mold by a robotic arm, and a 15 mm diameter center hole (called a stacking ring) is created. The time it takes to "stamp" one CD is usually two to three seconds.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This method produces the clear plastic blank part of the disc. After a metallic reflecting layer (usually aluminium, but sometimes gold or other metal) is applied to the clear blank substrate, the disc goes under a UV light for curing and it is ready to go to press. To prepare to press a CD, a glass master is made, using a high-powered laser on a device similar to a CD writer. The glass master is a positive image of the desired CD surface (with the desired microscopic pits and lands). After testing, it is used to make a die by pressing it against a metal disc.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The die is a negative image of the glass master: typically, several are made, depending on the number of pressing mills that are to make the CD. The die then goes into a press, and the physical image is transferred to the blank CD, leaving a final positive image on the disc. A small amount of lacquer is applied as a ring around the center of the disc, and rapid spinning spreads it evenly over the surface. Edge protection lacquer is applied before the disc is finished. The disc can then be printed and packed.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Manufactured CDs that are sold in stores are sealed via a process called "polywrapping" or shrink wrapping.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1995, material costs were 30 cents for the jewel case and 10 to 15 cents for the CD. Wholesale cost of CDs was $0.75 to $1.15, which retailed for $16.98.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cd_costs_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cd_costs-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On average, the store received 35 percent of the retail price, the record company 27 percent, the artist 16 percent, the manufacturer 13 percent, and the distributor 9 percent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cd_costs_59-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-cd_costs-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When 8-track tapes, cassette tapes, and CDs were introduced, each was marketed at a higher price than the format they succeeded, even though the cost to produce the media was reduced. This was done because the apparent value increased. This continued from vinyl to CDs but was broken when
       <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">
        Apple
       </a>
       marketed MP3s for $0.99, and albums for $9.99. The incremental cost, though, to produce an MP3 is very small.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Writable_compact_discs">
        Writable compact discs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Writable compact discs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Recordable_CD">
        Recordable CD
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Recordable CD">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maxell_CD-R_700MB_40x_20040321.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="600" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Maxell_CD-R_700MB_40x_20040321.jpg/220px-Maxell_CD-R_700MB_40x_20040321.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Maxell_CD-R_700MB_40x_20040321.jpg/330px-Maxell_CD-R_700MB_40x_20040321.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Maxell_CD-R_700MB_40x_20040321.jpg/440px-Maxell_CD-R_700MB_40x_20040321.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maxell_CD-R_700MB_40x_20040321.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         700
         <a href="/wiki/Mebibyte" title="Mebibyte">
          MiB
         </a>
         CD-R next to a
         <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_pencil" title="Mechanical pencil">
          mechanical pencil
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/CD-R" title="CD-R">
        CD-R
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Recordable Compact Discs,
       <a href="/wiki/CD-R" title="CD-R">
        CD-Rs
       </a>
       , are injection-molded with a "blank" data spiral. A photosensitive dye is then applied, after which the discs are metalized and lacquer-coated. The write laser of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CD_recorder" title="CD recorder">
        CD recorder
       </a>
       changes the colour of the dye to allow the read laser of a standard
       <a href="/wiki/CD_player" title="CD player">
        CD player
       </a>
       to see the data, just as it would with a standard stamped disc. The resulting discs can be read by most CD-ROM drives and played in most audio CD players. CD-Rs follow the
       <i>
        Orange Book
       </i>
       standard.
      </p>
      <p>
       CD-R recordings are designed to be permanent. Over time, the dye's physical characteristics may change causing read errors and data loss until the reading device cannot recover with error correction methods. The design life is from 20 to 100 years, depending on the quality of the discs, the quality of the writing drive, and storage conditions. However, testing has demonstrated such degradation of some discs in as little as 18 months under normal storage conditions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-14_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-14-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoMR-15_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AutoMR-15-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This failure is known as
       <a href="/wiki/Disc_rot" title="Disc rot">
        disc rot
       </a>
       , for which there are several, mostly environmental, reasons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The recordable audio CD is designed to be used in a consumer audio CD recorder. These consumer audio CD recorders use SCMS (
       <a href="/wiki/Serial_Copy_Management_System" title="Serial Copy Management System">
        Serial Copy Management System
       </a>
       ), an early form of
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_rights_management" title="Digital rights management">
        digital rights management
       </a>
       (DRM), to conform to the AHRA (
       <a href="/wiki/Audio_Home_Recording_Act" title="Audio Home Recording Act">
        Audio Home Recording Act
       </a>
       ). The Recordable Audio CD is typically somewhat more expensive than CD-R due to lower production volume and a 3%
       <a href="/wiki/Audio_Home_Recording_Act#AHRA_royalties" title="Audio Home Recording Act">
        AHRA royalty
       </a>
       used to compensate the music industry for the making of a copy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McFadden_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McFadden-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       High-capacity recordable CD is a higher-density recording format that can hold 90 or 99 minutes of audio on a 12 cm (5 in) disc (compared to about 80 minutes for
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       audio) or 30 minutes of audio on an 8 cm (3 in) disc (compared to about 24 minutes for
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       audio).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The higher capacity is incompatible with some recorders and recording software.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdrfaq_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cdrfaq-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="ReWritable_CD">
        ReWritable CD
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: ReWritable CD">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/CD-RW" title="CD-RW">
        CD-RW
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/CD-RW" title="CD-RW">
        CD-RW
       </a>
       is a re-recordable medium that uses a metallic alloy instead of a dye. The write laser in this case is used to heat and alter the properties (amorphous vs. crystalline) of the alloy, and hence change its reflectivity. A CD-RW does not have as great a difference in reflectivity as a pressed CD or a CD-R, and so many earlier CD audio players
       <i>
        cannot
       </i>
       read CD-RW discs, although
       <i>
        most
       </i>
       later CD audio players and stand-alone
       <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">
        DVD
       </a>
       players can. CD-RWs follow the
       <i>
        Orange Book
       </i>
       standard.
      </p>
      <p>
       The ReWritable Audio CD is designed to be used in a consumer audio CD recorder, which will not (without modification) accept standard CD-RW discs. These consumer audio CD recorders use the
       <a href="/wiki/Serial_Copy_Management_System" title="Serial Copy Management System">
        Serial Copy Management System
       </a>
       (SCMS), an early form of
       <a href="/wiki/Digital_rights_management" title="Digital rights management">
        digital rights management
       </a>
       (DRM), to conform to the United States'
       <a href="/wiki/Audio_Home_Recording_Act" title="Audio Home Recording Act">
        Audio Home Recording Act
       </a>
       (AHRA). The ReWritable Audio CD is typically somewhat more expensive than CD-RW due to (a) lower volume and (b) a 3%
       <a href="/wiki/Audio_Home_Recording_Act#AHRA_royalties" title="Audio Home Recording Act">
        AHRA royalty
       </a>
       used to compensate the music industry for the making of a copy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McFadden_64-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-McFadden-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Speed">
        Speed
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Speed">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Due to technical limitations, the original ReWritable CD could be written no faster than 4x speed. High Speed ReWritable CD has a different design, which permits writing at speeds ranging from 4x to 12x. Original CD-RW drives can only write to original ReWritable CDs. High Speed CD-RW drives can typically write to both original ReWritable CDs and High Speed ReWritable CDs. Both types of CD-RW discs can be read in most CD drives. Higher speed CD-RW discs, Ultra Speed (16x to 24x write speed) and Ultra Speed+ (32x write speed) are now available.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Copy_protection">
        Copy protection
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Compact_disc&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Copy protection">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CD/DVD_copy_protection" title="CD/DVD copy protection">
        CD/DVD copy protection
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       audio specification, except for a simple "anti-copy" statement in the subcode, does not include any
       <a href="/wiki/Copy_protection" title="Copy protection">
        copy protection
       </a>
       mechanism. Known at least as early as 2001,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BadCDs_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BadCDs-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       attempts were made by record companies to market "copy-protected" non-standard compact discs, which cannot be ripped, or copied, to hard drives or easily converted to other formats (like
       <a href="/wiki/FLAC" title="FLAC">
        FLAC
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/MP3" title="MP3">
        MP3
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Vorbis" title="Vorbis">
        Vorbis
       </a>
       ). One major drawback to these copy-protected discs is that most will not play on either computer CD-ROM drives or some standalone CD players that use CD-ROM mechanisms. Philips has stated that such discs are not permitted to bear the trademarked
       <i>
        Compact Disc Digital Audio
       </i>
       logo because they violate the
       <i>
        Red Book
       </i>
       specifications. Numerous copy-protection systems have been countered by readily available, often free, software, or even by simply turning off automatic
       <a href="/wiki/AutoPlay" title="AutoPlay">
        AutoPlay
       </a>
       to prevent the running of the DRM
       <a href="/wiki/Executable" title="Executable">
        executable
       </a>
       program.
      </p>

           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation web">

           </a>

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Inventor+of+the+Week+-+James+T.+Russell+-+The+Compact+Disc&amp;rft.pub=MIT&amp;rft.date=1999-12&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fweb.mit.edu%2Finvent%2Fiow%2Frussell.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACompact+disc">

         </span>
